The squid is cut, obviously.  Some people have actually said they like this ending better than the ending of the comic.  I won't go that far, I will simply say I love both endings equally.    There were some things I did not like about this movie.  One problem was the action scenes.  The action scenes are spectacular, but they are not really supposed to be.  Every action scene from the book is blown up in this movie.  Reference, the fight scene in the jail.  In the book, there is one panel that shows Nite Owl and Silk Spectre each knocking out one guy.  In the movie, they take on about a dozen guys.  But they didn't make this an action movie, primarily, so it is not a huge problem.  I thought they made the movie a little too violent in some scenes.  Some examples are the part where Rorschach kills the pedofile, the mugging scene, or the part where one of the guys working for Big Figure inexplicably has a chain saw and he cuts...yeah.  I wish they hadn't felt the need to put the word F**k in almost every line of dialogue.  And that big sex scene in the ship did absolutely nothing to move the story line along.  This is a great adaptation, but the violence and language could have been toned down a little bit.

He has even received the ultimate accolade this week namely a vicious attack from the increasingly insane ex Trot and current bigot Peter Hitchens who blamed him for causing more damage than the Iraq War and described him as \\"a debauched, capering streak of living gristle who ought to be exhibited as a warning to the young of what drugs can do to you\\". As usual Hitchens couldn't be more wrong since after reading \\"Life\\" a electrifying autobiography ghost written with James Fox someone ought to work out the physiology of Richards since the man is clearly indestructible despite the most astounding chemical intake and even more remarkable he appears to going as strong as ever. The life of this man who founded the Rolling Stones, invented rock guitar, gave us \\"Honky Tonk Women\\", \\"Brown Sugar\\", the seminal \\"Exile on Main Street\\" and a host of other treasures is something we should warmly celebrate and not carp about.<br /><br />Great rock autobiographies are a rare species but this book by Richards amounting 547 pages ranging from a drug bust in Fordyce, Arkansas to a quick final explanation that he did indeed snort his Dad's ashes (but in a very affectionate way!) and ending the death of his dear old mum Doris is a very intimate, revealing, warts an all account of a fascinating life packed with brilliant photographs and stories to spare. Fox has captured his subject well and you can hear Richards voice loud and clear with its colourful language of \\"cats\\", his love of Shepherd's pie (\\"don't bust the crust\\") and roguish charm. You will not be surprised that a large part of the book deals with Richards copious pharmaceutical use. Indeed with parts of his memory wiped out sections of the text are given over to the first hand remembrances of family members and friends like Waddy Watchel, Don Was and his great mate Bobby Keys which are often very harrowing. The legendary Freddie Sessler \\"Keith's second dad\\" is a key figure here. This is a man who described himself as \\"the worlds oldest groupie\\", got Keith out of \\"scrapes\\" and supplied his drugs including pharmaceutical cocaine graphically described in a passage on page 373. Richards knows that he was lucky to survive all this hedonism and the poignancy of his remarks when he tells us stories about the deaths of fellow travellers like Billy Preston and Gram Parsons are all the more pronounced and sad for it. The fact that his co-conspirator Ronnie Wood navigated this madness particularly with a his own \\"freebasing\\" crack cocaine indulgences which Richards highlights from 1980 onwards is another example of the \\"get out of jail\\" philosophy of life employed by the two most colourful members of the Stones.<br /><br />At the core of the book is the Jagger/Richards relationship which has gone through phases of almost tender brotherly love to intense visceral hatred (listen to \\"Had it with you\\" on Dirty Work which charts the nadir of this phase). The cleaned up Richards circa 1980s \\"Emotional Rescue\\" cannot today forgive Jagger's attitude on \\"his return\\" who had \\"fallen in love with power\\" and whose constant put downs of him are still very raw. As he states \\"the phrase which rings in my ears all these years later is \\"Oh, shut up Keith\\". Things gradually improved over the years and despite Jagger's Knighthood (\\"the Mick I grew up was a guy who'd say shove your little honours up your ar*e\\") by 2004 Richards and Jagger were working a closely as ever and he accepts \\"you've got to go through the bulls**t; its like a marriage\\"<br /><br />The book charts all the great Richards myths, the blood changing, the skull ring, the tax exile in France, the falling out of the tree incident and the Toronto drug bust in huge detail which finally led to him giving up heroin. The part however which I especially enjoyed was his early years around Dartford and Sidcup and his passion for the blues particularly Jack Elliot and the impact of Elvis. The generosity throughout to the great Charlie Watts who clearly is the glue that holds the Stones together, and a great loss to the United Nations Peace Keeping Corps, is genuine and full of love. Watts survival from cancer is emotionally charted by Richards and his relief tangible that Watts came back. And then there is the music not just with the Stones but Keith's side projects like the X-pensive Wino's which is a tale well told; while his relationship with Anita Pallenberg and its impact on the construction of the Stones greatest song \\"Gimme Shelter\\" is fascinatingly unveiled.<br /><br />The life of Keith Richards is a chronicle of the ultimate rock survivor and icon. Frankly he should not be here and the fact that he never sleeps means he has been here \\"longer\\" than the average 66 year old. Despite yourself you can't help but be absorbed by the myth and legend of the man, his bluntness and his often outspoken nonsense. Let us be frank anyone who calls his dog \\"Syphilis\\" must have something going for him. Consequently when in a hundred years time someone sits down and writes the definitive history of rock music it should start with the sentence that \\"In the beginning was the riff and the riff was with Keith\\".around Dartford and Sidcup and his passion for the blues particularly Jack Elliot and the impact of Elvis. The generosity throughout to the great Charlie Watts who clearly is the glue that holds the Stones together, and a great loss to the United Nations Peace Keeping Corps, is genuine and full of love. Watts survival from cancer is emotionally charted by Richards and his relief tangible that Watts came back. And then there is the music not just with the Stones but Keith's side projects like the X-pensive Wino's which is a tale well told; while his relationship with Anita Pallenberg and its impact on the construction of the Stones greatest song \\"Gimme Shelter\\" is fascinatingly unveiled. Frankly he should not be here and the fact that he never sleeps means he has been here \\"longer\\" than the average 66 year old. Consequently when in a hundred years time someone sits down and writes the definitive history of rock music it should start with the sentence that \\"In the beginning was the riff and the riff was with Keith\\".

B0048IMZMG|5.0|In Taylors third studio album, she seems determined to continue to blur the lines between pop, country and rock. She also pushes her   immense vocal talent to previously un-reached levels of passion. Since each song is so unique, I'll go through them individually.      \\"Mine\\"- The first single off of this album has already seen massive amounts of radio play, and although it's still great, it's one of the   least interesting songs on the album. Telling the story of a girl who's parents divorced, it explores her feelings for her boyfriend, but   with constant fear that their relationship will end like her parents. It ends with her boyfriend telling her that he loves her just as   much as she loves him, and they he'll never leave her. While a charming song, it doesn't have much of a \\"new\\" feel to it, as it would've    fit just as well into her \\"Fearless\\" album.      \\"Sparks Fly\\"- Reminiscent of her previous song \\"The Way I Loved You\\" it deals with a roller-coaster relationship where both parties realize   that it's somewhat dysfunctional but their passion for each other is too great for them to stop being together. It is also the first song   with a taste of Taylors increased maturity and her inclusion of sexuality into her songs. It drops her previous fairy-tale-esque portrayal of   relationships in favor of a more adult and passionate romance.      \\"Back to December\\"- Rumored to be about her short-lived romance with Taylor Lautner, it is a regretful song apologizing for how she behaved at the   end of their relationship. She says she'd go back in time to change it if she could, and hopes that he can forgive her. Probably my least favorite   song on the album, it's pretty repetitive and not exceptionally interesting lyrically, it's one of those songs that you feel like you've heard before.      \\"Speak Now\\"- This song breaks the albums trend of being grounded in reality, it's essentially a musical take the classic wedding interruption   seen in movies like \\"While You Were Sleeping\\". Taylor sings about hiding in a wedding and then coming out at the last second to tell the groom   that the bride is the wrong girl, and he shouldn't marry her.Not the best song on the album, but it's certainly competent.      \\"Dear John\\"- Seemingly doing a complete 360 from \\"Back to December\\" this song appears to be about John Mayer, but instead of apologizing to him   she accuses him of taking advantage of her youthful naivete. The chorus to this song is hauntingly beautiful, and really shows some of her   \\"new\\" voice that she uses on this album, hitting much richer highs than her lighter tones from previous albums. It's actually quite a bit like   Miley Cyrus's voice, albeit with much more lyrical polish.      \\"Mean\\"- Probably Taylors most spiteful song to date, it carries itself with an air of cocky nonchalance. Where it really shines it's   ability to put to music, a theme that everyone has had at sometime in their life. In the lyric \\"Someday I'll be living in a big old city,    and all you're ever gonna be is mean\\" it sums up the feeling of moral superiority we all feel over someone who is being a jerk. It really comes    from the heart, which is where Taylor shines.      \\"The Story Of Us\\"- Starting off with a catchy guitar riff, this song is also a great example of her newer style of singing. Telling the   story of two people who fight, but then proceed to ignore each other rather than work out their problems. \\"This is looking like a contest,   of who can act like they care less, but I liked it better when you were on my side\\" It should really hit home with her teenage crowd, and is   a really well executed song.      \\"Never Grow Up\\"- It starts off seemingly from the perspective of a child's mother, who sings about how she wishes her kid wouldn't grow up   and be hurt by the troubles of adult life. About halfway through the song it switches to the perspective of the child who is feeling lonely   in her apartment in the city, wishing she'd nevergrown up. A really sweet song similar to \\"The Best Day\\" from Fearless.      \\"Enchanted\\"- Not very complicated lyrically, but has a really nice instrumental accompaniment. A showcase for her ability to do haunting, slow songs   so sweet they border on lullaby.       \\"Better Than Revenge\\"- One of the most interesting songs on the album, it has a harder rock feel than anything else she's made. If you didn't know   it was Taylor, you could easily confuse it for Avril Lavigne, and it has a very similar theme to her friend Hayley Williams' song \\"Misery Business\\".   It also has pretty risque lyrics for the typically angelic singer, such as \\"She's not a saint and she's not what you think. She's an actress, whoa,she's better known for the things that she does   on the mattress, whoa.\\" It's nice to see her branching out into other genres, and succeeding.       \\"Innocent\\"- This song came off as fairly pathetic at the VMA's, sort of patronizing Kanye West for what amounted to fairly small incident. Even f you ignore the background to it, it's   not one of her best pieces. Really the only \\"skip\\" song I have on this album.      \\"Haunted\\"- An interesting song, the title and intro to it seem to be on the album mostly to coincide with the nearly-Halloween release date. It's got a lot of electric guitar,   and is sort of Taylors take on a rock power ballad, it stumbles lyrically a few times, but it's a cool song nonetheless.      \\"Last Kiss\\"- I really wish this was the last song on the album, instead of \\"Long Live\\" since it's both a much better song, and gives a much better sense of completion. Similar to \\"Enchanted\\" and her older   \\"Come In With The Rain\\" it's basically Taylor doing what she does best. Slow, crooning, and lyrically beautiful it's one of my favorite songs from her, period.      \\"Long Live\\"- This song seemed kind of weird to me, it seems to be almost like a rapper calling out members of his \\"crew\\" and talking about how they've conquered the \\"the game\\". I came to theconclusion that it's about her struggle to become famous, and how she'll be rembered. The most religious song I've heard her sing, it mentions \\"the kingdom's lights shining just for me and you\\"   it's sort of interesting, but like I said earlier, \\"Last Kiss\\" would've felt better as an ending to me.      Overall, if you're a Taylor Swift fan, you've probably already bought this and you'll like everything on it. If not, buy a few of the singles and let it soak in. If you really like them, buy up the rest of her albums and you won't be dissapointed!

B00003CXIP|3.0|\\"Stand By Me,\\" sometimes confused with \\"Lean On Me,\\" is both a coming of age and nostalgia story. The main characters are 12-year-old boys, but it is rated R so many parents might not want to watch it with their 12-year-old kids.<br /><br />As a coming of age story, it's pretty rough - the school of hard knocks - and I don't say this because of the dead body. All of the adults are seriously dysfunctional, all of the kids are either abused or neglected. Among the four boys, only Gordie and Chris have a solid friendship. Gordie, a thoughtful and understandably insecure boy, is believable, but Chris is too knowing, wise, and supportive for a kid, so at times it seems he is Gordie's adult mentor more than his kid friend. Any kid would benefit from a friend like Chris (indeed, any adult would benefit from a friend like Chris!). I point this out because when I watch a coming of age story, I expect to see kids coming of age rather than acting like wise adults facing tough challenges. I can cheer for kids who act like wise, brave, and strong adults, but I can't quite believe it. Chris and Gordie's superstar brother (John Cusack) seemed a bit too supportive of Gordie's writing to be believable; it's more likely they would have teased him about it. Sure, it made me feel all warm and fuzzy to see that kind of support, but it was overdone and got sappy.<br /><br />It's a movie, so I know that a lot has to happen to keep things interesting. But as a story, it seems a little much. In the space of a couple of days, the boys run into the local gang of violent hoodlums, an attack dog, a train, and a dead body. If such exciting things happened to the average kid, there'd be a lot of great stories to tell. I guess dysfunctional parents and bad childhoods have their benefits, while good, safe childhoods are boring by comparison. :-) Conveniently enough for the story, the feud between the boys and the violent gang is left unresolved. B002C6K7N0|5.0|I was delighted with The System Has Failed, the band's tenth studio album which was a step in the right direction as Megadeth altered their direction back to a more thrash sound again. It wasn't just the musical style however, as the songs were great, the musicianship was excellent and the production was punchy again. The next album, United Abominations took that ball and ran with it, mixing the style from their thrash era with the style found on Countdown To Extinction to create a modern sounding album that still had that return to roots feel.<br /><br />The band's twelfth album, Endgame, feels like the next logical step in the new era; getting that next bit heavier and faster than United Abominations but without becoming stale or uninteresting. The album opens up with an enjoyable instrumental speed metal track full of guitar solos. The phrase `start as you mean to continue,' comes to mind as the rest of the album follows that formula, with the exception of `The Hardest Part Of Letting Go...' which is similar in style to `Promises,' from their The World Needs a Hero album, only reworked with some extra speed metal segments.<br /><br />There are no weak tracks, no bad guitar solos and the production is brilliant, really there isn't a lot more you could expect. Dave Mustaine snarls and barks in top form and even lets his singing voice out on some of the more melodic moments, his voice is as good as it ever was, with no slide or decline to be found. Furthermore the drumming is rock solid, with plenty of double kick and inter cymbal fills to accompany the bevy of flawless guitar solos and bouncy bass guitar lines.<br /><br />Standout tracks include the amazing thrash style title track `Endgame,' which is probably the catchiest song on the album, `This Day We Fight,' an awesome straight up thrash number and the interesting closer `The Right To Go Insane,' which has a kind of `Youthanasia,' feel to it.<br /><br />To summarize, Endgame is Megadeth at the peak of their abilities, with top notch production, musicianship and songwriting. If you liked the last two Megadeth albums then you will unquestionably love this well written masterpiece.on, musicianship and songwriting. If you liked the last two Megadeth albums then you will unquestionably love this well written masterpiece.

Having experienced the sudden death of his beloved, eleven-years-older wife, Tropper's protagonist Doug Parker is left in the grief-stricken position of being a twenty-something widower.  Not only is he unsure how to handle it, others in his circle are as well.<br /><br />The book picks up one year after Doug's wife's death.  Doug remains mired in his grief.  Family members are trying - one way or another - to get him to move along.  The next few months of Doug's life - the focus of this book - prove to be very messy indeed.  The book is equal parts grief and comedy.<br /><br />I'm surprised that 'Widower' hasn't been made into a movie.  At many points in the novel, it reads more like a screenplay.  I have to think Tropper wrote the book with a screenplay in mind.  Most notably, I imagine every teenage actor in Hollywood itching to play Doug's 16-year-old stepson, Russ. B002ZFQD0Y|5.0|Serj Tankian's Elect The Dead Symphony is certainly an interesting release. At first, the idea for an artist with just one album releasing a live album seemed like a bad idea, then when you add in the idea of Serj's short three minute songs getting the orchestral treatment the idea seemed even more ill-advised, it led me to make unpleasant assumptions about the man's ego.<br />I'm glad to report however, that the problems I perceived are largely absent and Serj was fully justified in releasing this symphonic live concert.<br />As well as songs from the Elect The Dead album, there are two brand new songs `Gate 21,' and `The Charade,' as well as two more non album tracks `Blue,' and `Falling Stars.'<br />Another reason that this concert works so well is that the songs have been pretty severely rearranged to suit the Auckland Philharmonia Orchestra, it seems more like an MTV Unplugged version of Elect the Dead was given the symphony treatment than the existing versions. There is no distorted guitar whatsoever, no bass guitar and no drum kit as all percussion is handled by the orchestra.<br />The concert's greatest strength is also its greatest weakness. This concert relies a great deal more on the Orchestra than any other Rock/Orchestra cross over I've ever seen, which while making it unique and impressive may alienate some fans who were expecting a mixture of the band and the orchestra more in line with Kiss's Alive 4 or Metallica's S&M orchestral concerts.<br />Some songs suit the style more than others, with the chorus of `Money,' for example, seeming clumsy. Songs like `Elect the Dead,' and `Saving Us,' however are amazing and truly feel grand with the powerful backing of the Orchestra.<br />In terms of the DVD, the visuals, camerawork, direction and editing are spot on, they really couldn't have done a better job its almost worth watching just as a demonstration on how to correctly make a concert DVD. The sound is similarly perfect, the only place where quality comes into play are your individual opinions on the arrangements and how well Serj's voice carries live. In my opinion, Serj nails it here with a faultless performance that in some instances surpasses the original album versions, but a few listeners may not be so convinced.<br />Overall, Elect The Dead Symphony is a very good concert, with top notch sound and visuals. If you feel that a stripped down version of the songs augmented by a huge Orchestra is for you then give it a try.ndividual opinions on the arrangements and how well Serj's voice carries live. 142311339X|4.0|Rick Riordan's The Lost Hero picks up shortly after his Percy Jackson series ended and continues onward in the same universe with both new and familiar characters. Actually, I should say \\"mostly\\" the same universe, as Riordan has broadened his Greek mythology premise outward to include the Roman gods as well (or as is often the case, the familiar Greek gods in their less-familiar Roman aspects).     Percy literally isn't around for this one (don't worry--he appears to play a major role in the next); he's gone missing and nobody knows what happened to him. Instead, The Lost Hero begins with Jason, a young boy who finds himself on a school bus with his two \\"friends\\" Leo and Piper, save that he has no memory of who they are, how he got there, or who he is. Just as he's piecing together they belong to the Wilderness School--an institution for \\"bad kids\\"--the group is attacked by wind spirits, which Jason mysteriously seems to recognize. In the melee, Jason learns he can fly, has a nifty magical sword, and has pretty good skills. Eventually he and his two friends are rescued by Annabeth (looking for Percy) and brought back to Camp Half-Blood, where they learn who they are:  Jason the son of Zeus, Piper the daughter of Aphrodite, and Leo the son of Hephaestus.   Jason, it appears, has had his memories stolen by one of the gods, though what does leak out is strange in that he seems to be more associated with Rome than Greece. He isn't the only one with issues though. Piper has been asked to betray Camp Half-Blood to save her father. And Leo has a hidden ability somehow connected to his mother's death. Before the friends can fully settle in, they become wrapped up in a quest and a Great Prophecy which will see them travel the country battling monsters, demons, and gods in order to avert disaster.       All of which should sound familiar to readers of the first series, and there is a lot that is familiar here, for both good and bad. As in the earlier series, Riordan does a nice job with the small details that bring the gods to life. This is not a richly detailed world much as one finds in Harry Potter or LOTR; Riordan's books have always been much more about pace and action. But when he does slow a bit to bring the world more fully before us, we get some great quick moments, such as Argos of the many eyes crying or when we're brought into the home of Boreas the god of the North Wind.     The action is fast and nearly omni-present, though there are pacing issues. The book, well over 500 pages, could easily have lost a 100 or so I'd say. Some action scenes are drawn out (either because they move a bit predictably or because they become repetitive) and there are several odd moments where characters recapitulate the action in their heads, action we've already seen so as a reader I was wondering why I was being told this again.     Riordan's trademark humor is here, as is his facile ability to present a realistic teen point of view. A few times the humor might feel a bit forced or the attempt to show the characters as TEENS too obvious, but these moments are rare; mostly this feel to be a book about teens by someone who actually knows teens, rather than by an author who has an \\"idea\\" of teenhood in his/her mind (or worse, memory).     Jason's lost memories are played in too contrived a fashion, welling to the surface at convenient moments, and too many characters have that annoying \\"I know what is going on but for your own good can't tell you\\" sense of knowledge. Finally, the Roman mythology is a truly interesting and relatively original concept, but it feels a bit too shadowy for too much of the book (far less so in the last quarter) and feels therefore also a bit contrived--pieces of information left out by the author so we can get to a big reveal or set scene at the end.     Notwithstanding those problems, the books speeds along, much as the Percy Jackson ones did and it's hard to imagine young readers not jumping aboard and crying out for \\"more, more\\" at the end of this just as they did at the end of The Lightning Thief. Despite the issues mentioned, these books feel more solid, composed by a more confident and assured author. Even better, Riordan aims a bit darker here than in earlier books, dealing with a lot of parental issues of all sorts and personal demons of guilt and despair. This gives the new series the potential to be a bit deeper than the original (the idea of being \\"moved\\" by emotion rather than simply \\"moved along\\" by plot). And the early Percy books had much the same issues and improved.     Riordan may not have the crossover youth-adult appeal that some YA authors manage, his books lacking that richness or depth or subtlety. But one can't argue with his success with the youth market. The Lost Hero I'm sure will be no exception. As such, highly recommended for that group, with hopes he tightens things up for the next.

B002PEP4U0|5.0|A debate has been raging for years within that rarefied global community that earns its keep from the business of what we Americans call \\"foreign aid.\\" (Others, less afflicted by an aversion to international engagement, call the field \\"overseas development assistance.\\")<br /><br />On one side are the advocates for large-scale bilateral and multilateral aid, insisting that huge grants from the World Bank, the International Monetary Fund, the U.S. Agency for International Development, and their ilk are the only source of real hope for the many desperately poor nations of what is broadly, though incorrectly, called the Global South (Asia, Africa, and Latin America). The advocate-in-chief for this perspective is Professor Jeffrey Sachs of Columbia University, who has argued that massive infusions of aid to the governments of the poorest nations can lift them out of poverty in short order. In 2006, Sachs published his seminal book, The End of Poverty: Economic Possibilities for Our Time, a work that provided the rationale for the Millennium Development Goals.<br /><br />Arrayed against Sachs and his colleagues are the born-again critics of government-to-government aid, most noticeably William Easterly, a long-time World Bank economist who came in from the cold in recent years to testify to the widespread failure of \\"foreign aid.\\" His 2007 book, The White Man's Burden: Why the West's Efforts to Aid the Rest Have Done So Much Ill and So Little Good, set off the debate between the two opposing camps.<br /><br />The gist of the difference between the two perspectives is simple: One side insists that the problem of poverty is far too big to be addressed through anything other than large-scale action carried out within each poor country on a national scale. The other side contends that top-down, nationwide development programs rarely work and that only solutions crafted at the grassroots and adopted by those who are most affected by them can bring about genuine social change.<br /><br />Though I've read a number of other books taking one side or another in this debate, the work that has cast the most light on the topic is one that paid no attention whatsoever to \\"foreign aid\\" or economic development schemes, whether large or small. It's an extraordinary, first-person tale by a young man from Malawi entitled The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind.<br /><br />William Kamkwamba, the narrator of this awe-inspiring story, was a seventh-grade dropout who mastered fundamental physics by reading an out-of-date English textbook in a local, three-shelf library near his village and using his knowledge to construct a working windmill out of junkyard parts to generate electricity to irrigate his father's farm. He was 14 years old.<br /><br />You can read news reports and even the most perceptive magazine articles about the challenges of development, but you won't get nearly as close to the essential truth of the challenge as you will from reading The Boy Who Harnessed the Wind. Kamkwamba's tale is unsparing of himself, his community, and his country. Through his all-seeing eyes, we witness the tragic consequences of the profound official corruption that held sway in Malawi for so many years after it gained its independence from Britain in 1964. We feel the unrelenting hunger he and his family experienced for months on end in the famine of 2001-2002. We see the darkness descend all around us as William is hounded by fearful villagers who can only explain his windmill as magical. But, most of all, we observe the steady evolution of his brilliant young mind as he confronts one setback after another, and prevails over them all.<br /><br />If there is hope for Africa, as I firmly believe, it lies in the minds and hearts of William Kamkwamba and other young people whose innate genius is unlocked by the spread of education and opportunity for self-expression at the grassroots. There are hundreds, maybe thousands, of William Kamkwambas across Sub-Saharan Africa. Andit will be a combination of top-down aid - to build schools, train teachers, and buy textbooks - with the local action of countless NGOs, with both local and international support, that will provide them with the tools and the freedom to solve the problems that have held down their forebears for generations past. I don't think genuine development - thorough-going social change - will come any other way.<br /><br />(From Mal Warwick's Blog on Books)ools, train teachers, and buy textbooks - with the local action of countless NGOs, with both local and international support, that will provide them with the tools and the freedom to solve the problems that have held down their forebears for generations past. 0140157352|4.0|Getting to Yes is one of the foundational texts for negotiation. The book states that there are two main ways of negotiating: first, by position, or second, out of interest.<br /><br />Positional negotiation is based on fixed, opposing position. and tends to result in compromise (or no agreement at all). Negotiation from position is when you lock into a fixed position: For example, a man offers $10 for a rug. The seller asks for $30, so the customer offers $15. The seller offers $25, and the customer offers $20. The seller agrees. So the customer pays $10 more than he originally wanted and the vendor receives $10 less.<br /><br />Interest-based negotiation tries to get at the underlying interest, not the position. A classic example is if two little girls both want an orange. Instead of cutting the orange in half, their mother serves asks about interests. One girl wants to eat the meat of the orange, and the other wants the peel to use in baking. By negotiating from interests, creativity can result in each getting all of what they want.<br /><br />By trying to separate the people from the problem, Getting to Yes helps negotiators analyze each problem on their own merits, independent of people's perceptions, attributions, and relationships.

B003XD7ECI|5.0|`Frontiers records' has come up with some rather original ideas for melodic albums but this one is really genius. `Kiske - Sommerville', as the title suggests and most are already aware, is a melodic hard rock/metal album based on vocals duets of legendary German vocalist Michael Kiske and female vocal authority Amanda Somerville.<br /><br />I suppose there is not much reason to go into details regarding the abilities and approaches of the two singers as they are both metal idols at their own right while their contribution on this album has been very much explored by other reviewers.With that in mind I would like to stand on the other factors (beyond Michael and Amanda) that make this album such a musical success and for that matter are just as important as the names on the front cover.<br /><br />A key success factor was bringing to the project possibly the most capable songwriting core of `Frontiers records' that of bassist Mat Sinner and guitarist Magnus Karlsson. Both have been known for delivering amazing melodic works in the genres of melodic AOR, hard rock and metal; and the collaboration of the seasoned Mat with the younger Magnus ideally balances old school and modern influences on the album. The album features additional songwriting by Keyboard player Jimmy Kresic and Amanda, the material they come up with is more mid tempo and symphonic/orchestra-oriented. So closing the songwriting part, the listener does enjoy a variety of rock and metal styles throughout `Kiske/Somerville' while all songs are consistent in big melodies and stellar production.<br /><br />In terms of performing line-up the project finds Matt Sinner on bass guitar, Magnus Karlsson on lead guitar, Sander Gommans also on lead guitar, Jimmy Kresic on keyboards while drum duties are divided between Ramy Ali and Martin Schmidt.<br />Instrument-wise this project continues to surprise as Sinner's bass is louder and more aggressive than one would expect, Magnus' solos are instantly recognizable and never compromised in their length, while the drumming is far more intense than anyone would have anticipated, referring not only to the double bass attack but to the very technical stick work that is to be found throughout the album.<br /><br />Yes, \\"Kiske-Somerville\\" does capture the best of Michael Kiske and Amanda Somerville still it goes on to offer the listener much more that simply an all star project. It is impossible to find any fault on this album:<br />1)The concept is original 2) The songwriting is diverse yet consistently melodic 3) the singers live up to their reputation while uncovering much more of their vocal abilities than expected 4) the performance on instruments unforgettable and 5) it is all topped by possibly the greatest production ever.<br /><br />The limited edition features a 12th bonus track `Set Afire' and a DVD with the two breathtaking promo videos for `If I had a wish' and `Silence'.their length, while the drumming is far more intense than anyone would have anticipated, referring not only to the double bass attack but to the very technical stick work that is to be found throughout the album. 0007263678|5.0|So much has been written about World War II that one is astonished to find new material and a new slant on this dramatic and frightening story.  Here, with Finest Years, Max Hastings, the former editor-in- chief of the Daily Telegraph, tackles the war from the actions, speeches and thoughts of Winston Churchill.  It was not a straight line towards greatness for Churchill.  He dealt with divided opinion about the continuation of struggling against Nazi Germany in the early days of his Prime Ministership, with substantial segments of the aristocracy urging appeasement.  The story begins on that fateful day, May 10, 1940, Churchill's first day in office and the day in which Germany began the onslaught in the West, ending only weeks later in the capitulation of France.  Germany had been preparing for war since the mid-1930's and had already absorbed Austria, Czechoslovakia, and Poland, each with relative ease.     None of the histories of the war that i have read treat the intricate relationship between Churchill and Roosevelt with the same degree of nuance and detail that Hastings devotes to this issue.  It was, in fact, a critical relationship, deftly handled by Churchill, an intensely egocentric man but one who was able to move beyond his ego to gradually persuade the American president of the common cause against Germany.  it took all of Churchill's immensely powerful charm, sense of history and language, and dogged persistence to move Roosevelt to broaden American support for Britain as it battled Germany on the Western front.  The relationship began to subtly shift as American might became increasingly evident in the North African campaign and in the invasion of southern Europe.  American forces rapidly outnumbered those of Britain.  The runup to the invasion of Western Europe, in June 1944, was designed by Americans, over Churchill's strenuous objections, and led by a spectacularly successful American general, Dwight Eisenhower.      But in the early days, when the fate of the West hung by a thread, Churchill was supremely capable of rallying his country in resisting Hitler.  The performance of the Royal Air Force in reducing the toll exacted by German bombs was critical.  Churchill was everywhere during the summer of 1940 as the fight for the skies over Britain unfolded.      However, his record was far more spotty in running the day-to-day affairs of Britain.  Political opposition, dormant during the early days of the war, gained strength as Britain tired of its long struggle and Churchill's sense of military strategy and tactics, famously deficient since the Gallipoli disaster in World War I, became more obviously questionable.    Hasting treats the battle on the Eastern front -- the German invasion of Russia beginning in the summer of 1941 -- with great care.  Stalin was the essence of the crafty leader. He was willing to spend enormous amounts of Russian blood to defend his country, ultimately a brilliantly successful effort.  In fact, the battle in the East became the determining struggle in the war, with Russia losing more than 20 million men killed in action.  This eventually exhausted the German army and, although it gained strength and striking force after its withdrawal from Russia, it never regained the fighting power that it had in the early days of the war.      This is a marvelous book.  It sheds so much new light on the leader of wartime Britain.  Hastings sums up his view of Churchill, a man who had towering talents and some very human faults, by noting \\"the sustained magnificence of his performance.\\"

B002LC8HF0|5.0|Like many, I first noticed Brandon Sanderson's name as the author chosen by Robert Jordan's widow to complete his epic (slight understatement) series.  As someone who grew up reading CS Lewis, JRR Tolkien, and Stephen R Lawhead, I somehow managed to never begin the Wheel of Time series or read the works of many other authors who have taken up the mantle of Tolkien.  But since I deployed to Afghanistan with my Kindle, I thought why not start now?      Thus I began with \\"The Way of Kings,\\" which quickly became one of my top five favorite books (I even got my wife to read it--and enjoy it, despite the fact that I can't even get her to watch LoTR or Star Wars!), and after finding myself missing Kaladin, Shallan and Dalinar, and knowing that it would be a while before Book 2 arrived, I found myself perusing the reviews of Sanderson's other works and arrived at the Mistborn Trilogy.    One of the most endearing qualities of Sanderson in this series is how much forethought went into the first two books.  In retrospect, the clues that were placed in the first book were not completely realized until the end of the third.  Secondly, as someone with disdain for gratuitous violence and sex scenes, I was refreshed by the lack of offensive material in Sanderson's works.      But most of all, Sanderson creates believable characters, compelling backstories, logical frameworks for magical abilities and a geography/climate/weather system that keep the plot moving excitedly forward.  I found that even the antagonists had reasonable motives and though you still feel disdain for them, you are given an understanding of them that almost affords a bizarre sense of empathy.  In his antagonist development, Sanderson really soars.    Finally, it's nice when one can't easily predict every plot twist and trick.  There's a great mix of external and internal forces that propel the plot along.      Weaknesses:   I don't know why authors of trilogies/series always feel compelled to remind the reader frequently of previous books' salient events.  It sometimes feels patronizing.  Does anyone ever start these series in the middle?    Minor, but in the second book, on the Kindle, there are dozens of scanning typos.  Such as instead of \\"Ham,\\" \\"Flam\\" is used.  It's just a little distracting.  I didn't find this to be the case in the first or third book.    At any rate, I highly recommend this trilogy and can see why Jordan's widow chose Sanderson after reading these three books.      Next up, Elantris.    Also, speaking of starting a series mid-stream, I want to read the Wheel of Time; but am not excited about reading some of the slower books in the middle.  Can any of the slower books be skipped altogether?  I am primarily reading the series just to get to Sanderson's works at the end.

B003VANOFY|3.0|First the good news.    1) The overall sound quality is excellent. I hear things in my music and games that I didn't even know were there until now. The headset sounds better than anything I've ever used in the past. That said, I'm no audiophile and this is the most expensive headset I've ever bought.    2) The surround sound effect is excellent. I had to take the headset off more than once because I was convinced that the sounds were coming from my 5.1 home theater sound system (my computer is hooked into a A/V receiver). I love watching movies and playing games with this headset.    3) I find it's looks and design appealing.    4) The wireless range is excellent. I can talk on skype or listen to music anywhere in my two-story house.    5) The headset also worked in a linux virtual machine via virtualbox. I haven't tried a bare-metal linux install, nor have I tried using it with a mac.    6) People have said that my voice sounds perfectly clear on skype.    7) The headset wraps around my ears which isolates outside noise very well.      And now the bad news.    1) The logitech control panel doesn't reliably indicate when the headset is being charged and when surround sound is detected. Reboots and reinstalls sometimes don't fix the problem. I've since given up on using the control panel altogether and regularly check for updates.    2) The headset is supposed to give you a little beep to warn you when the batteries are running low, but does not always do so.    3) A high-pitched noise can be heard in the left ear when the headset is used while charging. It is a minor annoyance to me, but some may find it aggravating. Rarely, I hear a very loud and frightening electronic noise while using it and charging at the same time - more reboots, reinstalls, to fix that.    4) The included usb accessory that acts as a charging station and hub for the wireless transmitter/receiver does not reliably charge the headset. Sometimes the headset cannot be turned on when it is charging via the charging station. I've had to resort to using a separate charging cable.    5) Configuring the headset so that you can hear your own voice while talking will be difficult for some people.    6) Even the newer beta version of the software package does not fix all the issues.    7) I have a somewhat large head and ears, and find the headset to be uncomfortable to wear for more than a few hours.    8) Buying something that is broken and hoping it will be fixed in the future would be unwise.    In summary, I had high expectations given my past experiences with logitech products and feel very ambivalent about this headset. The software and/or drivers need a lot of work. Hopefully a forthcoming software update will get things running solid. If you aren't a technical user who is willing and able to cope with this device's problems, then don't buy it.    FYI - I'm using this product on Windows 7 64bit.    Update: As recommended on the logitech user forums, I've quit using the charging accessory/usb hub, and plugged the transceiver directly into the back of my computer. It seems that many of the problems have now gone away.

Though it draws on the band's doom metal roots (complete with growls), it's got its fascinating, on-edge feel that's both unsettling and oddly compelling at the same time. Something about this song grabbed me the very first time I heard it and still hasn't let me go -even more than the dramatic \\"Jillian (I'd Give My Heart).\\"    \\"Destroyed\\" (B-side) is an interesting and somewhat flat inclusion. It kind of blended into the rest of the album, much like \\"Aquarius,\\" \\"Forsaken\\" and \\"It's the Fear.\\" I'd say that's the only minor issue I had with this album -some of the songs just merged together and lost some of their individuality. I would have like to see a little more distinction between each song. I was also a little sad to see that the U.S. version of The Silent Force didn't include European bonus tracks \\"A Dangerous Mind,\\" \\"The Swan Song\\" or even the beautiful iTunes exclusive (and B-side) \\"Say My Name.\\"    The song \\"Somewhere\\" is also interesting to note here. When I first heard it on the album I found it uninspiring and dull and just lumped it in with the songs that all sounded alike. However, ever since WT released the duet version on Black Symphony and An Acoustic Night at the Theatre between Sharon and Anneke Van Giersbergen (of The Gathering fame), it's breathed new life into the song and I now find it to be one of the most intriguing and emotional pieces on the album.    Though better things do come in the future for WT, The Silent Force is a wonderful achievement for the band and the genre. Though it has some flaws, at it's heart it's an emotional, dramatic display of some of the best female-fronted metal out there.

B003XRDYX2|4.0|I must admit that I was skeptical about this set when I first heard about its upcoming release.  It seemed to me that Columbia was cashing in on the success of the Beatles' mono set last year.  My doubt arose from the fact that, unlike the Beatles (especially McCartney), Bob has always been one to favor the spirit of the moment in recording, and never one to look back.  That said, I am not convinced that he had the patience to sit through the extensive trial and error required to create these mono mixes (which is what Columbia wanted us to believe).    However, I decided to give the \\"Best Of\\" disc a shot.  Upon my first listen when it arrived last week, I became convinced of the value of this release and decided to go with the whole set.  Even though Bob probably had little to do with the original production (aside from providing notes in response to an acetate copy of \\"Blonde on Blonde\\" while on tour in '66), it is worth giving ample credit to Hammond, Halee, Johnston, etc. for their efforts to present his work so well.    I would say that the mono mix of \\"Freewheelin'\\" alone justifies the purchase of this set.  The subtle expressive nuances in Bob's voice are much more upfront when juxtaposed directly against the downstroke of his guitar.  And it is also nice to hear Bob's spontaneous vocal asides during the harmonica breaks in \\"Bob Dylan's Blues\\" uninterrupted by the engineer trying to keep up on the panning dials!    Other highlights include the warmth of Bob's Nick Lucas Special in \\"Mr. Tambourine Man\\" (which had been starkly flattened in stereo), the clarity of the nylon string guitars in \\"Just Like a Woman\\" and \\"Fourth Time Around,\\" the art of guitar weaving in \\"Leopard Skin Pillbox Hat,\\" and the punch of the rhythm section in \\"Subterranean Homesick Blues,\\" \\"You Go Your Way and I'll Go Mine,\\" \\"She Belongs to Me,\\" and \\"Absolutely Sweet Marie\\" (which lost its power when separated into stereo).  I agree with others' comments that McCoy's and Buttrey's musicianship on \\"John Wesley Harding\\" appear much stronger in mono, and I would add that the same can be said for Strzelecki's bass playing on \\"Blonde on Blonde\\" and Gregg's drumming on \\"Back Home\\" and \\"Highway 61.\\"    Perhaps the weakest of the mono mixes is \\"Another Side.\\"  Wilson's reverb makes Bob's voice sound synthetically trebly at times (this is not aided by the fact that it was mixed at a slightly accelerated speed, making the songs shorter and higher than normal pitch).  In comparison with the stereo mix, the clarity of his guitar playing becomes muffled, the harmonica can be overwhelming, and the overall sound feels cloistered.  Also, on several of the mono electric tracks (i.e., \\"Love Minus Zero\\") the incidental percussion feels overaccentuated and distracts from the mood of Bob's poetry.  (It seems that the intent was to appeal to the mid-60s pop market; however, to my ears it just doesn't seem to have stood the test of time.)    Pricewise, I will admit that I am still bugged by Columbia's hype.  This set really only offers five (not eight) albums previously unavailable officially on CD in mono (Bob's first album and \\"The Times\\" were presented in mono on the 2005 remasters, as was \\"Another Side\\" on the old 1990s CD), and at a higher price per album.  Furthermore, it is hard to overlook the inattention to detail in calling this set the \\"original mono recordings.\\"  For example, the first mono pressings of \\"Highway 61\\" featured an alternate take of \\"Buick 6,\\" and the first pressings of \\"Blonde on Blonde\\" included Claudia Cardinale's photo in the gatefold.      All in all, though, I still would suggest going with the whole set (vs. just downloading individual albums).  The LP cover replicas and the booklet are a nice touch (even if a little unevenly cut/folded at times), and a CD played through a good set of speakers always trumps playback from an iPod.  There's enough going on here to justify sitting down and *listening* for a while.

B0013ND30M|5.0|Fauna, one of the three Good Fairies says that tearfully to her two companions Flora and Merriwether just before the film's ending.  Me, too, love happy endings that is, this is one of the better ones, young lovers reunited for happily ever after, and good triumphing over evil!  \\"Sleeping Beauty\\" was the first Disney animated film I had ever seen during its original run back in 1959 when I was just about five years old.   As such I have a fondness for it, and it is one of my favorite Disney animated films!    The lavish painstaking care and attention that went into this film from Walt Disney on down is evident in this masterpiece of animated art.  Set in the 14th century, the movie looks like a medieval illuminated manuscript come to life.   The colors are vivid, dazzling vibrant and glowingly lit from within like the finest jewels, with a spectrum that embraces all the hues of a rainbow and then some.   The soaring, lyrical musical score is the most classical of all the Disney animated films, excepting \\"Fantasia\\", adapted by George Bruns from the ballet \\"Sleeping Beauty\\" composed by the master composer Peter Illich Tchaikovsky.   This is a wonderful introduction to the world of classical music for children; I remember years later when I saw Tchaikovsky's \\"Sleeping Beauty\\" ballet performed I instantly flashed back to this movie at particular moments, chuckling to myself when I recognized Maleficent's theme in the \\"Puss'n'Boots\\" divertissement.    The story is so well known it almost doesn't bear repeating, it's based on the French version by Charles Perrault \\"La Belle au Bois Dormant\\", about the beautiful Princess Aurora who is cursed on her christening day to prick her finger on the spindle of a spinning wheel and die before the sun sets on her sixteenth birthday by an evil fairy who is angry she was excluded from the celebration.   This is softened by one of the good fairies attending who changes it to sleep not death, until she is awakened by the kiss of her truelove.   Disney supplied his own Americanized twists and tweaks to the tale.  These include the prerequisite cute woodland creatures as friends in the forest for Briar Rose/Princess Aurora. But hey they work, and we all like them!    The most inspired touches are the Three Good Fairies, each as individual as her signature color, Flora the born leader in her coral pink, the sweetly dreamy Fauna clad in leaf green garments, and the feisty little Merriwether whose always wears a vivid blue, these are natural extensions of the Fairy Godmother in Disney's earlier \\"Cinderella\\"; as a matter of fact Disney veteran actress Verna Felton supplies the voice for both the Fairy Godmother and Flora! The other most memorable improvement is the arch villainess, the perfectly named Maleficent, a menacing, Gothic figure in her horned headdress and flowing robes of black and purple.   Masterly voiced in curdled, disdainful tones by Eleanor Audley, she is one of the all time best in the Disney pantheon of villains.         There are two sequences that are my personal favorites.  The first is a lighthearted charming one where the Three Good Fairies use their magic to prepare for a sixteenth birthday party for Briar Rose.    Flora uses her wand to make a beautiful gown, Fauna to make a scrumptious birthday cake complete with candles, and Merriwether cleans the cottage.   Inanimate objects spring to life in a delightfully graceful ballet as scissors, needle and thread collaborate to sew the dress, the eggs crack themselves, and the flour, sugar, milk and spices measure themselves into a mixing bowl, and pour the batter into a splendidly tiered cake, while Merriwether leads the broom and mop in a happy parade across the room as they tidy up.   This is accompanied by a delicate, tinkling melody, that strengthens in tone and quickens it's leisurely pace as Flora and Merriwether engage in a comic magic duel as to what color Briar Rose's dress will be, Flora wants pink and Merriwether of courseblue, and the dress keeps changing color back and forth with each flash of their wands.   The second is the escape of Prince Philip from the Maleficent's grim dungeon assisted by the Three Good Fairies.   This is a riveting, heart racing flight, as a little kid I was on the edge of my seat in anxious anticipation, wondering would he make it safely back to King Stefan's castle to awaken Princess Aurora.   The camera angles, the imaginative touches, and particularly the musical score which builds in intensity and timbre, becoming jagged, frenzied and dissonant to emphasize the danger and the terror as Prince Philip bravely fights his way to the castle, through a maze of oversized lethal looking thorns only to have to battle with Maleficent herself transformed into a huge, horrific fire-breathing dragon combine to create an unforgettable scene.   For my money, this still remains one of the most breathtaking battles between good and evil ever presented on the screen.     The vocals are all exceedingly well done; Mary Costa has a lovely radiance in both speaking and singing that make her an enchanting Briar Rose/Princess Aurora, while Bill Shirley brings an engaging youthful manliness to Prince Philip.   I've already complimented Ms. Audley earlier on her Maleficent, so let me acknowledge the three actresses that bring the Good Fairies to life:  Verna Felton as Flora, Barbara Jo Allen as Fauna and Barbara Luddy as Merriwether; all three do sterling work.   Both Misses Felton and Luddy were Disney animation film veterans, who had worked successfully for Disney before and would again afterwards.      This is chock full of extra features mostly on disc 2.   There are games for the children, a music video, an alternative opening and deleted songs (a wise choice by Disney after viewing them) and a virtual reality tour of the Disneyland Sleeping Beauty Castle.   What I found most worthwhile was the \\"Making of Sleeping Beauty\\" featurette, a short subject on the composer Tchaikovsky and the commentary by Pixar animator and director John Lasseter, film critic and historian Leonard Maltin and Disney animator Andreas Dejas.   Their discussion and comments over the film were very interesting, and I especially liked Lasseter's briefly becoming a little boy again in his excitement over the battle between Prince Philip and Maleficent.   The one flaw is the many trailers and previews that are included, there must be close to 10 at least, very obnoxious, since you can't just access the menu to start the movie but have to keep skipping to the next one.    This is a very poor way to present a classic, & I don't think Walt Disney would have stood for it!
B003YP7N9O|5.0|For all practical purposes, Stand Up may as well be Jethro Tull's debut album (it isn't, just to clarify), as it establishes three precedents that would be followed on every Tull album since:<br /><br />1) Ian Anderson runs the show, and well that he should, as his songwriting gifts and overall vision took this band to amazing heights.<br /><br />2) Martin Barre's versatile guitar playing, while not as technically stupendous here as it would become later on, contributes a tuneful, bluesy edge to the proceedings.<br /><br />3) British Isles folk, as well as world music, would give this band a rich musical vein to mine for decades after.<br /><br />The music on Stand Up is truly unique. From the Jimi Hendrix meets Roland Kirk blues rock of A New Day Yesterday, the wistful beauty of the folk-based Reasons for Waiting and Look into the Sun, the affectionate, jazzy Bach parody of Bouree, to the Pakistani rock (yes, you read that right) of Fat Man, Anderson's songs sounded like no one else!<br /><br />This Collector's Edition set also includes a full 1970 Carnegie Hall concert (well, it's missing some stage banter, but only a pedantic dork like me will notice), with a great-sounding fresh remix, and some incredible performances. John Evans' piano recital includes bits of Beethoven, Gershwin, and Debussy, while Ian Anderson's atonal flute solo can only be described as iconoclastic.<br /><br />The bonus tracks include a mono version of the hit Living in the Past, the complete version of underrated B-side 17, as well as some interesting John Peel radio sessions. The DVD includes the Carnegie Hall concert in surround-sound, and a 45-minute Ian Anderson interview.<br /><br />By all means, if you're a Tull fan, buy this! If you're new to this band, you can't do much better than to start with Stand Up. It's the template that Tull used to create a whole slew of classics in the 70's. And it all started with this one!

B0040UAYI4|1.0|UPDATE - COD BLACK OPS:  Since this is the latest iteration of Activision's franchise, the review should be geared towards that game for the sake of new technology.  I tried out the eagleeye on Call of Duty: Black Ops a few days via the mouse/keyboard setup I previously had.  All worked well, but there were some issues with the start button randomly going off and the R1 turbo function locking up my fire key.  To keep it short:  It still works as before, but no turbo function.  Many people on Penguin United's forums complained of similar issues, and are asking for a firmware update to solve it.       ORIGINAL REVIEW:    Like most PC players, I was absolutely thrilled when I saw the demo of the PS3 Eagle Eye mouse and keyboard adapter in action.  There are several demos on youtube of people using it, as well as avid fans on Penguin's forums who absolutely love this product.  If you love Modern Warfare 2, or any Call of Duty game for that matter (i.e. you pre-ordered Black Ops months ago), you will love this contraption as well. However there is a major flaw with the hardware consumers should be worried about if they decide to pick this up.      Right out of the box, the PS3 Eagle Eye adapter comes with a mini cd for the drivers, and a very long 10 foot cord.  The box says most HID compliant mice or keyboards should work, but honestly it is a craps shoot as to what perpetuals will be recognized.  The G15, G11, and G19 wired keyboards by Logitech all didn't work, however Razer products such as the n52te and Naga mouse all worked perfectly. The Logitech Revolution wireless laser mouse worked with the eagle eye, however the M505 didn't.  It is best to check Penguin's forums before buying this product to get a good gauge on what might be compatible, however Penguin stresses on the website there are no guarantees. My guess is any product that is compatible with 1.1 USB should work, since the ps3 is 2.0 USB and slaved ports end up having to be 1.1 unless using a separate power source.  You also shouldn't be using any type of optical mouse either, only mice which have a default high dpi will do. Otherwise you may max out on your sensitivity settings and not be able to use the product well.    After setting up my Eagle Eye and firing up Modern Warfare 2 on PS3, the product worked as advertised.  Smooth 180 turns, excellent cross hair precision with no jutter or lag, honestly I was impressed by the ease at which I was playing online.  I no longer needed to buy games on Steam anymore, I'm joining the console kids!    After I was done with MW2, I picked up my second favorite shooter Battlefield Bad Company 2, and this is where I started running into issues.  The reticle started juttering a bit.  There wasn't any lag, just a pesky aim point that kept moving.  I tried some vehicle combat, which is critical when playing Battlefield and did alright, but noticed by 180 turning radius was severely limited now.  It was somewhat limited in character movement, but in vehicles, I was picking up the mouse several times just to do a 180.  This is with max sensitivity settings and a Razer Naga laser gaming mouse.  Medal of Honor was the same story, luckily vehicles aren't as crucial.    I tried the Eagle Eye on Borderlands, and while my aim point didn't move as much, as soon as I walked into a vehicle I was floored.  You CANNOT move the vehicle and aim at a different spot other than the front.  If you move your aim point, the vehicle will turn in that direction.  I went back and tried Battlefield with a chopper and noticed this glaring issue as soon as I took off, I couldn't fly the thing without an analogue stick on the left.  What this means for the consumer, is if the game developer decides to make vehicle control variable analogue on the left side, the PS3 Eagle Eye adapter will not work correctly.  There is no option in the Eagle Eye software to compensate for analogue movement.  You either get 100% or 0 deflection, just like a digital controller.    The marketing for Eagle Eye is misleading.  It gives the impression you will be able to play well on almost ANY shooter.  The reality is it was only demoed on MW2, thus you will get what you see on the videos, but little else.  Vehicle combat is sketchy, more so unusable.  Yes, you could argue that you wouldn't buy this device to drive, however vehicles are incredibly crucial in many shooters, thus the option to adapt to it must be there.  As for the aim point juttering on several games, well that's just poor hardware programming from Penguin's developers.   The glaring issue is Penguin doesn't stand by any compatibility quotes, leaving you with the dirty work of figuring out if your gear jives with the hardware. So in essence, you get a product that is telling you 60% of the time, it will work every time; not very reassuring.    There is hope in this product, as it is new and the developers take a keen interest in player feedback. But for a 59 dollar perpetual that only works well on one game, you need to ask yourself how much you really love Modern Warfare 2.

0792164911|5.0|There are only a handful of movies that I consider to be the true Halloween classsics... and \\"Sleepy Hollow\\" is one of them. Loosely adapted from Washington Irving's story, this Tim Burton classic is part romance, part detective story, and part gothic horror -- and Johnny Depp helps elevate it to a brilliant supernatural thriller.<br /><br />Constable Ichabod Crane (Johnny Depp) is sent to the tiny town of Sleepy Hollow to investigate a series of murders. He soon learns that the locals believe that a monstrous undead Hessian known as the Headless Horseman is the one who's beheading people. As a rational man of science, Ichabod thinks this is ridiculous... until he has a close encounter with the Horseman after he committed some of his murders.<br /><br />But Ichabod is still convinced that someone living is controlling the Horseman, and it's all wrapped up in a complex scheme involving a pregnancy, a marriage, a will, and a tangled family tree. But as deaths stack up and heads roll, Ichabod and his orphaned servant Masbeth (Marc Pickering) discover that they are up against evil that they couldn't have even imagined.<br /><br />\\"Sleepy Hollow\\" is vintage Tim Burton, back when he was a little more restrained about said style -- everything is painted in blacks, whites and greys with brief splashes of color (BLOOD!), eerie gothic buildings, and skeletal trees. And there's that little edge of rickety weirdness, especially when Depp whips out his buggy-eyed binoculars.<br /><br />Now, it's not perfect -- the witch's hentai-tongue/eyeballs always make me burst out laughing. But Burton really infuses this movie with a sense of creeping, bloody horror that gets more and more nightmarish, peppered with moments of bleak humor (Ichabod trying to throttle the Horseman) and some really disturbing beheadings. It's a pretty sharp contrast to the rather sweet, shy romance between Ichabod and his host's daughter, Katrina Van Tassel.<br /><br />As for Depp, he's absolutely perfect -- he plays Ichabod Crane as a young man with a horrifying past (which is revealed via dreams) and an abiding love for logic, science and reason. He's also pretty nervy, fragile and neurotic; how often do you see movie heroes who faint more often than the heroines?<br /><br />Christina Ricci is rather wooden at first, but she improves a lot as we discover some of the secrets she keeps hidden. Pickering is excellent as a young orphan who latches onto Ichabod as a surrogate father, and Miranda Richardson is all spidery creepiness as Lady Van Tassel. Also keep an eye out for Christopher Lee, Michael Gambon and Christopher Walken's hilariously over-the-top Horseman.<br /><br />\\"Sleepy Hollow\\" is all creepy atmosphere and weirdness, but it also has a solid story at its center. 0374158460|2.0|I love to read and usually finish a book within a few days to a week...if the tome is quite hefty. So when I catch an episode of Oprah and she declared \\"Freedom\\" to be one of the best books she has ever read, I was intrigued. I quickly purchased the book from Amazon and eagerly began to read it as soon as it arrived.    Firstly, the narrative was not engrossing or very well paced. The uneven writing style (i.e. jumping from author to character writing perspective) was quite discouraging. The pacing left great banal expanses of irrelevant information that did not advanced the plot or endear the characters.    Secondly, I enjoy broadening my vocabulary and don't mind an author inserting unfamiliar words that I might have to look up in the dictionary, but I despise it when an author makes up a word and uses it without definition as if it were of a common lexicon.     Finally, I am not a prude, but jumping from one sexual encounter to another incessantly does not make for great narrative. The graphic depiction of one sexual act after another that doesn't enhance understanding of the character is just unnecessary. I thoroughly enjoy a sexual encounter between characters that helps explain who they are (i.e. a rape that explains a closed off character or a consummation of a relationship), but I don't need to know the details in a grossly explicit way.    Overall, there was plenty of character development, to a fault, but I never really felt compassion for any of the characters. B003A2C3J6|4.0|I bought this book knowing I would probably agree with it. Turns out I was right.    Detox baths, ear candling, Brain Gym, homeopathy, the MMR scare, nutritionists, moisturisers, vitamin supplements, pharmaceutical companies  - they are all myths that are deftly and humorously deconstructed by physician and professional skeptic Ben Goldacre. Most importantly, he presents facts - quotes peer-reviewed papers, looks behind the news reports, reasons on the evidence. There is a fair dollop of intellectual snobbery throughout the book.     Skepticism is fine, even healthy to a point. It's foolish to swallow everything you hear. But hard-nosed skeptics usually have more faith than they care to admit - and Ben Goldacre has an almost evangelical faith in evidence-based medicine.     Don't get me wrong, I enjoyed the book. And it certainly confirmed some suspicions. I just worry about how Ben Goldacre's unrelenting skepticism has coloured his interpretations of the facts. In the end, books like these really only polarise people into opposing groups. I wish the author was more interested in dignifying the uninformed than bashing people with logic.

0553386816|4.0|Ford County (the setting for Grisham's first book \\"A Time to Kill\\") is the fertile ground from which John Grisham harvests seven short stories which touch the human condition in general and the southern condition in particular.     If you are not southern, these stories will provide you a window of understanding towards southern culture and how southerners deal with life as well as death.  If you are southern, you will recognize most of the characters contained within the pages of \\"Ford County\\".  If you are also from a small southern town (population less than 5,000), you will probably know each of them personally.     Grisham is at his best in this collection when the story has some legal underpinning, but is not the focus of the story itself.  The exception is the final story (Funny Boy) which handled a man dying of AIDS.  It was respectful and showed what I considered to be an even handed portrayal of small town reaction to such an event.    (Blood Drive) A tale of three less than noble volunteers heading to Memphis to donate blood to an injured local.  This is my least favorite as it becomes a \\"three hicks go to the big city and get into trouble\\" story.  I did not find it funny, but rather shook my head as I know both uneducated and educated people that have even less common sense than these three.    (Fetching Raymond) Two brothers and their mother visit the third son on death row.  The characters are well drawn and Grisham manages to subdue his own hatred of the death penalty, though is there for all to see.    (Fish Files) & (Casino) are basic legal scam stories.  Both are enjoyable but suffer from Grisham's tendency to allow a story to reach its end without enough uncertainty to provide dramatic tension.    (Michael's Room) is the revenge story every person who has been screwed over by a slick lawyer and the legal system dreams of over and over.  The story itself is satisfying, but the ending is completely unbelievable.    (Quiet Haven) My favorite story.  The hero of the story is out for himself, but he is like the thief who robs from thieves.  He redeems himself by doing more good than ill.  As someone with experience with nursing homes, I was rooting for him from the first page to the last.      All in all, \\"Ford County\\" is better than Grisham's recent novels.  Not a masterpiece, but a very good read.

1400064287|5.0|Summary   In \\"Made to Stick\\", the authors Chip and Dan Heath elaborate on the \\"stickiness factor\\" discussed by Malcolm Gladwell in \\"The Tipping Point\\", wherein some messages and ideas tend to stick in our minds more effectively than others.  The overall concept of the book discusses how the creation of \\"stickiness\\" can be attributed to six principles:  Simplicity, Unexpectedness, Concreteness, Credibility, Emotions, and Stories.  In fact, each chapter focuses on each one of these six factors individually.   The first chapter, \\"Simple\\", focuses on how for ideas to stick, they need to be simplified to their core ideas and made short and to the point.  Company slogans such as Southwest's \\"THE low-fare airline\\" are great examples of simple, sticky messages.  The second chapter, \\"Unexpected\\", discusses how ideas and messages that come as a surprise to those who hear them can be very sticky.  Factors that can further elaborate on this unexpectedness factor such as raising the target-audience's curiosity about the message can even further create stickiness in their minds.  Chapter three, \\"Concrete\\", elaborates on how messages that are solid and concrete rather than being scattered and abstract will be much more sticky and easily retained.  The authors discuss how abstract concepts tend to be confusing to those who aren't experts, and therefore are very unlikely to stick in the minds of the general population.  However, ideas and messages that are more concrete and tangible (as well as visual, in many cases) can be much stickier.  The example of a third grade teacher in Iowa is cited as an example in this chapter.  Because her students were too young to fully grasp why Martin Luther King Jr. had been assassinated, she conducted an experiment in which she separated the brown and blue eyed children into different groups and then told them different things/gave them different privileges based on their eye color.  This experiment was \\"brutally concrete\\" in demonstrating to thechildren what racism was and why MLK had been assassinated.  The fourth chapter, \\"Credible\\", talks about how credibility is essential for a message to stick.  If a message comes from a person or institution that does not hold obvious credibility within society, those hearing this message are likely going to question its validity.  Statistics, as well as compelling details can often increase stickiness via credibility.  Chapter five explains how emotion can evoke a great deal of stickiness in ideas and messages.  When people are able to relate to something, they will often times become more vested in its message and become emotionally vulnerable to it.  Chip and Dan cite how charities, such as those who try to raise money for needy Korean orphans or cruelly-treated animals, often arouse sympathy and compassion amongst people which in turn ultimately inspires them to act.  Finally, Chapter six, \\"Stories\\", describe how stories have the power to both inspire others to act, as well as stimulate thinking.  The increased stimulation that occurs in the brain when people listen to stories is what causes them to think harder and analyze these messages more deeply, therefore making these messages very sticky.  The authors cite the Jared story (how the famous Subway spokesperson lost nearly 250 lbs on a diet that involved simply exercising and eating Subway sandwiches) as a great story that has stuck in the minds of probably every American familiar with the Subway brand.    Response/Opinion   I thoroughly enjoyed \\"Made to Stick\\", by Dan and Chip Heath.  Not only did I agree with their six principles, but I also found myself relating to many of them in my own life.  I also really liked how the book was organized, elaborating on each of these six factors individually in a chapter of their own, with an introduction and a prologue as well.  Ironically enough, I think the book's \\"concrete\\" and organized layout helped its ideas to stick with me, possibly more than any of the other books we have read thus far during this semester.   Through reading \\"Made to Stick\\", I felt that I could personally relate especially to the \\"Credibility\\" principle.  Since I am a very logical person, I tend to always find myself wondering why I should believe a particular message, as well as whether or not it comes from a credible source.  One example of how I can personally relate to the Credibility principle in this book was when I was actually applying for this MBA program last year.  During the application process, I knew that I wanted to attend a school in the Philadelphia area for financial reasons, as well as other factors.  However, I was a little wary as to which institutions would back my education with a credible name that would ultimately look good in the eyes of my future employers.  Though I had the general idea that schools like St. Joe's and Drexel offered solid and reputable programs, I had no concrete facts telling me of the true credibility of these curriculums.  However, once I found out for example that St. Joe's is ranked in the U.S. News and is also recognized by the Princeton review, I became much more confident in its credibility.  What I was reading and hearing about St. Joe's immediately began to stick in my mind to much more of a degree.  In fact, the credibility of St. Joe's became even more sticky when I consulted my friend's father, a very successful CFO with an impressive executive resume, about MBA credibilities.  As soon as he mentioned St. Joe's as one of the highly-credible schools, I was even more reaffirmed as to my believing what I had heard about the University.   Overall, I really enjoyed \\"Made to Stick\\" and felt that Chip and Dan Heath made some excellent points regarding ideas and messages' abilities to stick within our society.  Whether it be their simplicity and concreteness, or their ability to evoke emotion, an idea or message's success almost always depends on its ability to stick within society.  I think that these sixprinciples are excellent and encompassing factors representing what it takes to make a message or concept be retainable by those who hear it.
